{"type":"user","message":{"role":"user","content":[{"tool_use_id":"toolu_01RDsGedQtPv8XGVv6vj4LRF","type":"tool_result","content":"     1→= Hacking the compiler :camel:\n     2→\n     3→This document is a work-in-progress attempt to provide useful\n     4→information for people willing to inspect or modify the compiler\n     5→distribution's codebase. Feel free to improve it by sending change\n     6→proposals for it.\n     7→\n     8→If you already have a patch that you would like to contribute to the\n     9→official distribution, please see link:CONTRIBUTING.md[].\n    10→\n    11→=== Your first compiler modification\n    12→\n    13→1. Create a new git branch to store your changes.\n    14→+\n    15→----\n    16→git checkout -b my-modification\n    17→----\n    18→Usually, this branch wants to be based on `trunk`. If your changes must be on a\n    19→specific release, use its release branch (*not* the release tag) instead. For\n    20→example, to make a fix for 4.11.1, base your branch on *4.11* (not on *4.11.1*).\n    21→The `configure` step for the compiler recognises a development build from the\n    22→`+dev` in the version number (see file `VERSION`), and release tarballs and the tagged Git commits do\n    23→not have this which causes some important development things to be disabled\n    24→(ocamltest and converting C compiler warnings to errors).\n    25→\n    26→2. Consult link:INSTALL.adoc[] for build instructions. Here is the gist of it:\n    27→+\n    28→----\n    29→./configure\n    30→make -j 4\n    31→----\n    32→If you are on a release build and need development options, you can add\n    33→`--enable-ocamltest` (to allow running the testsuite) and `--enable-warn-error`\n    34→(so you don't get caught by CI later!).\n    35→\n    36→3. Try the newly built compiler binaries `ocamlc`, `ocamlopt` or their\n    37→`.opt` version. To try the toplevel, use:\n    38→+\n    39→----\n    40→make runtop\n    41→----\n    42→\n    43→4. Hack frenetically and keep rebuilding.\n    44→\n    45→5. Run the testsuite from time to time.\n    46→+\n    47→----\n    48→make tests\n    49→----\n    50→\n    51→6. You did it, Well done! Consult link:CONTRIBUTING.md[] to send your contribution upstream.\n    52→\n    53→See also our <<tips,development tips and tricks>>, for example on how to\n    54→<<opam-switch,create an opam switch>> to test your modified compiler.\n    55→\n    56→=== What to do\n    57→\n    58→There is always a lot of potential tasks, both for old and\n    59→newcomers. Here are various potential projects:\n    60→\n    61→* https://github.com/ocaml/ocaml/issues[The OCaml\n    62→  bugtracker] contains reported bugs and feature requests. Some\n    63→  changes that should be accessible to newcomers are marked with the\n    64→  tag link:++https://github.com/ocaml/ocaml/issues?q=is%3Aopen+is%3Aissue+label%3Anewcomer-job++[\n    65→  newcomer-job].\n    66→\n    67→* The\n    68→  https://github.com/ocamllabs/compiler-hacking/wiki/Things-to-work-on[OCaml\n    69→  Labs compiler-hacking wiki] contains various ideas of changes to\n    70→  propose, some easy, some requiring a fair amount of work.\n    71→\n    72→* Documentation improvements are always much appreciated, either in\n    73→  the various `.mli` files or in the official manual\n    74→  (See link:manual/README.md[]). If you invest effort in understanding\n    75→  a part of the codebase, submitting a pull request that adds\n    76→  clarifying comments can be an excellent contribution to help you,\n    77→  next time, and other code readers.\n    78→\n    79→* The https://github.com/ocaml/ocaml[github project] contains a lot of\n    80→  pull requests, many of them being in dire need of a review -- we\n    81→  have more people willing to contribute changes than to review\n    82→  someone else's change. Picking one of them, trying to understand the\n    83→  code (looking at the code around it) and asking questions about what\n    84→  you don't understand or what feels odd is super-useful. It helps the\n    85→  contribution process, and it is also an excellent way to get to know\n    86→  various parts of the compiler from the angle of a specific aspect or\n    87→  feature.\n    88→+\n    89→Again, reviewing small or medium-sized pull requests is accessible to\n    90→anyone with OCaml programming experience, and helps maintainers and\n    91→other contributors. If you also submit pull requests yourself, a good\n    92→discipline is to review at least as many pull requests as you submit.\n    93→\n    94→== Structure of the compiler\n    95→\n    96→The compiler codebase can be intimidating at first sight. Here are\n    97→a few pointers to get started.\n    98→\n    99→=== Compilation pipeline\n   100→\n   101→==== The driver -- link:driver/[]\n   102→\n   103→The driver contains the \"main\" function of the compilers that drive\n   104→compilation. It parses the command-line arguments and composes the\n   105→required compiler passes by calling functions from the various parts\n   106→of the compiler described below.\n   107→\n   108→==== Parsing -- link:parsing/[]\n   109→\n   110→Parses source files and produces an Abstract Syntax Tree (AST)\n   111→(link:parsing/parsetree.mli[] has lot of helpful comments). See\n   112→link:parsing/HACKING.adoc[].\n   113→\n   114→The logic for Camlp4 and Ppx preprocessing is not in link:parsing/[],\n   115→but in link:driver/[], see link:driver/pparse.mli[] and\n   116→link:driver/pparse.ml[].\n   117→\n   118→==== Typing -- link:typing/[]\n   119→\n   120→Type-checks the AST and produces a typed representation of the program\n   121→(link:typing/typedtree.mli[] has some helpful comments). See\n   122→link:typing/HACKING.adoc[].\n   123→\n   124→==== The bytecode compiler -- link:bytecomp/[]\n   125→\n   126→==== The native compiler -- link:middle_end/[] and link:asmcomp/[]\n   127→\n   128→=== Runtime system\n   129→\n   130→The low-level routines that OCaml programs use during their execution:\n   131→garbage collection, interaction with the operating system\n   132→(IO in particular), low-level primitives to manipulate some OCaml data\n   133→structures, etc. Mostly implemented in C, with some rare bits of\n   134→assembly code in architecture-specific files. The \"includes\"\n   135→corresponding to the `.c` files are in the link:runtime/caml[]\n   136→subdirectory.\n   137→\n   138→Some files are only used by bytecode programs, some only used by\n   139→native-compiled programs, but most of the runtime code is\n   140→common. (See `runtime_COMMON_C_SOURCES`, `runtime_BYTECODE_ONLY_C_SOURCES`,\n   141→and `runtime_NATIVE_ONLY_C_SOURCES` in link:Makefile[] for the list of common,\n   142→bytecode-only, and native-only source files.)\n   143→\n   144→See link:runtime/HACKING.adoc[].\n   145→\n   146→=== Libraries\n   147→\n   148→link:stdlib/[]:: The standard library. Each file is largely\n   149→independent and should not need further knowledge.\n   150→\n   151→link:otherlibs/[]:: External libraries such as `unix`, `threads`,\n   152→`dynlink` and `str`.\n   153→\n   154→Instructions for building the full reference manual are provided in\n   155→link:manual/README.md[]. However, if you only modify the documentation\n   156→comments in `.mli` files in the compiler codebase, you can observe the\n   157→result by running\n   158→\n   159→----\n   160→make html_doc\n   161→----\n   162→\n   163→and then opening link:./api_docgen/ocamldoc/build/html/libref/index.html[] in a web browser.\n   164→The documentation is located in\n   165→link:./api_docgen/odoc/build/html/libref/index.html[] when `--with-odoc` is\n   166→passed to the configure script.\n   167→\n   168→=== Tools\n   169→\n   170→link:lex/[]:: The `ocamllex` lexer generator.\n   171→\n   172→link:yacc/[]:: The `ocamlyacc` parser generator. We do not recommend\n   173→using it for user projects in need of a parser generator. Please\n   174→consider using and contributing to\n   175→link:http://gallium.inria.fr/~fpottier/menhir/[menhir] instead, which\n   176→has tons of extra features, lets you write more readable grammars, and\n   177→has excellent documentation.\n   178→\n   179→=== Complete file listing\n   180→\n   181→  BOOTSTRAP.adoc::        instructions for bootstrapping\n   182→  Changes::               what's new with each release\n   183→  CONTRIBUTING.md::       how to contribute to OCaml\n   184→  HACKING.adoc::          this file\n   185→  INSTALL.adoc::          instructions for installation\n   186→  LICENSE::               license and copyright notice\n   187→  Makefile::              main Makefile\n   188→  Makefile.common::       common Makefile definitions\n   189→  README.adoc::           general information on the compiler distribution\n   190→  README.win32.adoc::     general information on the Windows ports of OCaml\n   191→  VERSION::               version string. Run `tools/autogen` after changing.\n   192→  asmcomp/::              native-code compiler and linker\n   193→  boot/::                 bootstrap compiler\n   194→  build-aux/::            autotools support scripts\n   195→  bytecomp/::             bytecode compiler and linker\n   196→  compilerlibs/::         the OCaml compiler as a library\n   197→  configure::             configure script\n   198→  configure.ac::          autoconf input file\n   199→  debugger/::             source-level replay debugger\n   200→  driver/::               driver code for the compilers\n   201→  flexdll/::              git submodule -- see link:README.win32.adoc[]\n   202→  lex/::                  lexer generator\n   203→  man/::                  man pages\n   204→  manual/::               system to generate the manual\n   205→  middle_end/::           the flambda optimisation phase\n   206→  ocamldoc/::             documentation generator\n   207→  ocamltest/::            test driver\n   208→  otherlibs/::            several additional libraries\n   209→  parsing/::              syntax analysis -- see link:parsing/HACKING.adoc[]\n   210→  release-info/::         documentation and tools to prepare releases\n   211→  runtime/::              bytecode interpreter and runtime systems\n   212→  stdlib/::               standard library\n   213→  testsuite/::            tests -- see link:testsuite/HACKING.adoc[]\n   214→  tools/::                various utilities\n   215→  toplevel/::             interactive system\n   216→  typing/::               typechecking -- see link:typing/HACKING.adoc[]\n   217→  utils/::                utility libraries\n   218→  winpthreads/::          winpthreads submodule -- see <<winpthreads,further>>\n   219→  yacc/::                 parser generator\n   220→\n   221→[#tips]\n   222→== Development tips and tricks\n   223→\n   224→=== Keep merge commits when merging and cherry-picking Github PRs\n   225→\n   226→Having the Github PR number show up in the git log is very useful for\n   227→later triaging. We recently disabled the \"Rebase and merge\" button,\n   228→precisely because it does not produce a merge commit.\n   229→\n   230→When you cherry-pick a PR in another branch, please cherry-pick this\n   231→merge-style commit rather than individual commits, whenever\n   232→possible. (Picking a merge commit typically requires the `-m 1`\n   233→option.) You should also use the `-x` option to include the hash of\n   234→the original commit in the commit message.\n   235→\n   236→----\n   237→git cherry-pick -x -m 1 <merge-commit-hash>\n   238→----\n   239→\n   240→=== Code style\n   241→\n   242→Keep the style of the code you’re modifying. We don’t enforce the use of\n   243→automated formatters. For OCaml code,\n   244→https://github.com/OCamlPro/ocp-indent[ocp-indent] has been used.\n   245→We use https://editorconfig.org/[EditorConfig] for simple styling. Lots of\n   246→editors support EditorConfig\n   247→https://editorconfig.org/#pre-installed[out-of-the-box], or with\n   248→https://editorconfig.org/#download[plugins].\n   249→\n   250→[#opam-switch]\n   251→=== Testing with `opam`\n   252→\n   253→If you are working on a development version of the compiler, you can create an\n   254→opam switch from it by running the following from the development repository:\n   255→\n   256→-----\n   257→opam switch create . --empty\n   258→opam install .\n   259→-----\n   260→\n   261→If you want to test someone else's development version from a public\n   262→git repository, you can build a switch directly (without cloning their\n   263→work locally) by pinning:\n   264→\n   265→----\n   266→opam switch create my-switch-name --empty\n   267→opam pin add ocaml-variants git+https://$REPO#branch\n   268→----\n   269→\n   270→==== Incremental builds with `opam`\n   271→\n   272→This section documents some tips to speed up your workflow when you need to\n   273→alternate between testing your branch and patching the compiler.\n   274→We'll assume that you're currently in a clone of the compiler's source code.\n   275→\n   276→===== Initial setup\n   277→\n   278→For the rest of the section to work, you'll need your compiler to be\n   279→configured in the same way as `opam` would have configured it. The simplest\n   280→way is to run the normal commands for the switch initialization, with the extra\n   281→`--inplace-build` flag:\n   282→\n   283→-----\n   284→opam switch create . --empty\n   285→opam install . --inplace-build\n   286→-----\n   287→\n   288→However, if you need specific configuration options, you can also configure it\n   289→manually, as long as you make sure that the configuration prefix is the one\n   290→where `opam` would install the compiler.\n   291→You will then need to install the compiler, either from the working directory\n   292→(that you must build yourself) or using the regular sandboxed builds.\n   293→\n   294→-----\n   295→# Example with regular opam build\n   296→opam switch create . --empty\n   297→opam install .\n   298→./configure --prefix=$(opam var prefix) # put extra configuration args here\n   299→-----\n   300→\n   301→-----\n   302→# Example with installation from the current directory\n   303→opam switch create . --empty\n   304→./configure --prefix=$(opam var prefix) # put extra configuration args here\n   305→make -j\n   306→opam install . --assume-built\n   307→-----\n   308→\n   309→===== Basic workflow\n   310→\n   311→We will assume that the workflow alternates between work on the compiler and\n   312→external (`opam`-related) commands.\n   313→As an example, debugging an issue in the compiler can be done by a first step\n   314→that triggers the issue (by installing a given `opam` package), then adding\n   315→some logging to the compiler, re-trigger the issue, and based on the logs either\n   316→add more logging, or try a patch, and so on.\n   317→\n   318→The part of this workflow that we're going to optimize is when we switch from\n   319→working on the compiler to using the compiler. The basic way to do this is to\n   320→run `opam install .` again, but this will recompile the compiler from scratch\n   321→and also trigger a recompilation of all the packages in the switch.\n   322→\n   323→===== Using `opam-custom-install`\n   324→\n   325→The `opam-custom-install` plugin allows you to install a package using a custom\n   326→command instead of the package-supplied one. It can be installed following\n   327→instructions https://gitlab.ocamlpro.com/louis/opam-custom-install[here].\n   328→\n   329→In our case, we need to build the compiler, and when we've built everything\n   330→that we need then we run `opam custom-install ocaml-variants -- make install`.\n   331→This will make `opam` remove the previously installed version of the compiler\n   332→(if any), then install the new one in its stead.\n   333→\n   334→-----\n   335→# reinstall the compiler, and rebuild all opam packages\n   336→opam custom-install ocaml-variants -- make install\n   337→-----\n   338→\n   339→Since most `opam` packages depend on the compiler, this will trigger a\n   340→reinstallation of all the packages in the switch.\n   341→If you want to avoid that (for instance, your patch only adds some logging\n   342→so you expect the core libraries and all the already compiled packages to be\n   343→identical), you can use the additional `--no-recompilations` flag.\n   344→There are no checks that it's safe to do so, so if your patch ends up\n   345→changing even slightly one of the core libraries' files, you will likely\n   346→get inconsistent assumptions errors later.\n   347→\n   348→-----\n   349→# reinstall the compiler, leaving the opam packages untouched -- unsafe!\n   350→opam custom-install --no-recompilations ocaml-variants -- make install\n   351→-----\n   352→\n   353→Note about the first installation:\n   354→When you start from an empty switch, and install a compiler (in our case,\n   355→the `ocaml-variants` package provided by the compiler's `opam` file), then\n   356→a number of additional packages are installed to ensure that the switch\n   357→will work correctly. Mainly, the `ocaml` package needs to be installed,\n   358→and while it's done automatically when using regular `opam` commands, the\n   359→`custom-install` plugin will not force installation of dependencies.\n   360→Moreover, if you try to fix the problem by manually installing the `ocaml`\n   361→package, `opam` will try to recompile `ocaml-variants`, using the default\n   362→instructions. You can get around this by running\n   363→`opam reinstall --forget-pending` just after the `opam custom-install` command\n   364→and just before the `opam install ocaml command`.\n   365→Full example:\n   366→\n   367→-----\n   368→opam switch create . --empty\n   369→./configure --prefix=$(opam var prefix) --disable-ocamldoc --disable-ocamltest\n   370→make world && make opt\n   371→opam custom-install ocaml-variants -- make install\n   372→opam reinstall --forget-pending --yes\n   373→opam install ocaml\n   374→# You now have a working switch, in which you can start installing packages\n   375→-----\n   376→\n   377→One advantage of this plugin over a plain `make install` is that it\n   378→correctly tracks the files associated with the compiler, so if your\n   379→`make install` command only installs the bytecode versions of the tools,\n   380→then with `opam-custom-install` you will end up in a state where only the\n   381→bytecode tools are installed, whereas with a raw `make install` you will have\n   382→stale native binaries remaining in your switch.\n   383→Since it's significantly faster to build the bytecode version of the tools,\n   384→and many `opam` packages will pick the native version of the compilers if\n   385→present and the bytecode version otherwise, you can build your initial switch\n   386→with the native versions (to get quickly to a state where a bug appears),\n   387→then clean your working directory and start building bytecode tools only\n   388→for the actual debugging phase.\n   389→\n   390→===== Without `opam-custom-install`\n   391→\n   392→You can achieve some improvements using built-in `opam` commands.\n   393→\n   394→Using `opam install . --assume-built` will simply remove the\n   395→package for the compiler, then run the installation instructions\n   396→(`make install`) in the working directory, tracking the installed files\n   397→correctly. The main difference with the `opam-custom-install` version\n   398→is that there's no way to prevent this command from triggering a full\n   399→recompilation of your switch.\n   400→\n   401→You can also run `make install` manually, which will not trigger a\n   402→recompilation, but will not remove the previous version either and can\n   403→mess with `opam`'s tracking of installed files.\n   404→\n   405→=== Useful Makefile targets and options\n   406→\n   407→Besides the targets listed in link:INSTALL.adoc[] for build and\n   408→installation, the following targets may be of use:\n   409→\n   410→`make runtop` :: builds and runs the ocaml toplevel of the distribution\n   411→                          (optionally uses `rlwrap` for readline+history support)\n   412→                          (use `make runtop-with-otherlibs` if you need `Unix` or other\n   413→                           `otherlibs/` libraries)\n   414→`make natruntop`:: builds and runs the native ocaml toplevel (experimental)\n   415→\n   416→`make partialclean`:: Clean the OCaml files but keep the compiled C files.\n   417→\n   418→`make depend`:: Regenerate the `.depend` file. Should be used each time new dependencies are added between files.\n   419→\n   420→`make -C testsuite parallel`:: see link:testsuite/HACKING.adoc[]\n   421→\n   422→You can use `make foo V=1` to build the target foo and show full\n   423→commands instead of abbreviated names like OCAMLC, etc. This can be\n   424→useful to know the flags to use to manually rebuild a file.\n   425→\n   426→Additionally, there are some developer specific targets in link:Makefile.dev[].\n   427→These targets are automatically available when working in a Git clone of the\n   428→repository, but are not available from a tarball.\n   429→\n   430→=== Automatic configure options\n   431→\n   432→If you have options to `configure` which you always (or at least frequently)\n   433→use, it's possible to store them in Git, and `configure` will automatically add\n   434→them. For example, you may wish to avoid building the debug runtime by default\n   435→while developing, in which case you can issue\n   436→`git config --global ocaml.configure '--disable-debug-runtime'`. The `configure`\n   437→script will alert you that it has picked up this option and added it _before_\n   438→any options you specified for `configure`.\n   439→\n   440→Options are added before those passed on the command line, so it's possible to\n   441→override them, for example `./configure --enable-debug-runtime` will build the\n   442→debug runtime, since the enable flag appears after the disable flag. You can\n   443→also use the full power of Git's `config` command and have options specific to\n   444→particular clone or worktree.\n   445→\n   446→=== Speeding up configure\n   447→\n   448→`configure` includes the standard `-C` option which caches various test results\n   449→in the file `config.cache` and can use those results to avoid running tests in\n   450→subsequent invocations. This mechanism works fine, except that it is easy to\n   451→clean the cache by mistake (e.g. with `git clean -dfX`). The cache is also\n   452→host-specific which means the file has to be deleted if you run `configure` with\n   453→a new `--host` value (this is quite common on Windows, where `configure` is\n   454→also quite slow to run).\n   455→\n   456→You can elect to have host-specific cache files by issuing\n   457→`git config --global ocaml.configure-cache .`. The `configure` script will now\n   458→automatically create `ocaml-host.cache` (e.g. `ocaml-x86_64-pc-windows.cache`,\n   459→or `ocaml-default.cache`). If you work with multiple worktrees, you can share\n   460→these cache files by issuing `git config --global ocaml.configure-cache ..`. The\n   461→directory is interpreted _relative_ to the `configure` script.\n   462→\n   463→=== Bootstrapping\n   464→\n   465→The OCaml compiler is bootstrapped. This means that\n   466→previously-compiled bytecode versions of the compiler and lexer are\n   467→included in the repository under the\n   468→link:boot/[] directory. These bytecode images are used once the\n   469→bytecode runtime (which is written in C) has been built to compile the\n   470→standard library and then to build a fresh compiler. Details can be\n   471→found in link:BOOTSTRAP.adoc[].\n   472→\n   473→=== Speeding up builds\n   474→\n   475→Once you've built a natively-compiled `ocamlc.opt`, you can use it to\n   476→speed up future builds by copying it to `boot`:\n   477→\n   478→----\n   479→cp ocamlc.opt boot/\n   480→----\n   481→\n   482→If `boot/ocamlc` changes (e.g. because you ran `make bootstrap`), then\n   483→the build will revert to the slower bytecode-compiled `ocamlc` until\n   484→you do the above step again.\n   485→\n   486→=== Using merlin\n   487→\n   488→During the development of the compiler, the internal format of compiled object\n   489→files evolves, and quickly becomes incompatible with the format of the last\n   490→OCaml release. In particular, even an up-to-date merlin will be unable to use\n   491→them during most of the development cycle: opening a compiler source file with\n   492→merlin gives a frustrating error message.\n   493→\n   494→To use merlin on the compiler, you want to build the compiler with an older\n   495→version of itself. One easy way to do this is to use the experimental build\n   496→rules for Dune, which are distributed with the compiler (with no guarantees that\n   497→the build will work all the time). Assuming you already have a recent OCaml\n   498→version installed with merlin and dune, you can just run the following from the\n   499→compiler sources:\n   500→\n   501→----\n   502→./configure # if not already done\n   503→make clean && dune build @libs\n   504→----\n   505→\n   506→which will do a bytecode build of all the distribution (without linking\n   507→the executables), using your OCaml compiler.\n   508→\n   509→Merlin will be looking at the artefacts generated by dune (in `_build`), rather\n   510→than trying to open the incompatible artefacts produced by a Makefile build. In\n   511→particular, you need to repeat the dune build every time you change the interface\n   512→of some compilation unit, so that merlin is aware of the new interface.\n   513→\n   514→You only need to run `configure` once, but you will need to run `make clean`\n   515→every time you want to run `dune` after you built something with `make`;\n   516→otherwise dune will complain that build artefacts are present among the sources.\n   517→\n   518→Finally, there will be times where the compiler simply cannot be built with an\n   519→older version of itself. One example of this is when a new primitive is added to\n   520→the runtime, and then used in the standard library straight away, since the rest\n   521→of the compiler requires the `stdlib` library to build, nothing can be build. In\n   522→such situations, you will have to either live without merlin, or develop on an\n   523→older branch of the compiler, for example the maintenance branch of the last\n   524→released version. Developing a patch from a release branch can later introduce a\n   525→substantial amount of extra work, when you rebase to the current development\n   526→version. But it also makes it a lot easier to test the impact of your work on\n   527→third-party code, by installing a local <<opam-switch,opam switch>>: opam\n   528→packages tend to be compatible with released versions of the compiler, whereas\n   529→most packages are incompatible with the in-progress development version.\n   530→\n   531→\n   532→=== License\n   533→\n   534→The OCaml distribution in this repository, which includes the OCaml\n   535→compiler and runtime, the OCaml standard library, and some extra\n   536→tools, is licensed under the LGPL version 2.1, with a linking\n   537→exception. See link:LICENSE[] for the full license text. Using the\n   538→https://spdx.org/licenses/[SPDX] standard for license identifiers,\n   539→this is the license \"LGPL-2.1 WITH\n   540→https://spdx.org/licenses/OCaml-LGPL-linking-exception.html[OCaml-LGPL-linking-exception]\".\n   541→Let us include here a few explanations about the intent of this\n   542→licensing choice -- note that only the license text has legal value.\n   543→\n   544→The broad intent of the LGPL is that if you modify the OCaml\n   545→distribution, and distribute your modified version to your users, then\n   546→you have to provide the source code of your version -- the LGPL, like\n   547→the GPL and unlike the BSD and MIT licenses, is \"hereditary\"; modified\n   548→versions of LGPL programs must remain free software under the same\n   549→license. (For more general discussion of the LGPL license, see the\n   550→https://www.gnu.org/licenses/gpl-faq.en.html[FAQ] maintained by the\n   551→GNU project.)\n   552→\n   553→On the other hand, if your code is not derived from the OCaml\n   554→distribution, it only uses it, then you can use the licensing terms of\n   555→your choice. We use an explicit \"linking exception\" precisely to make\n   556→it clear that it is fine to link statically or dynamically with parts\n   557→of the OCaml distribution, typically the OCaml runtime and the\n   558→standard library. Linking exceptions are a common practice for\n   559→language compilers and runtime libraries, for example the GCC project\n   560→uses such an exception. See the\n   561→https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/GPL_linking_exception[Wikipedia article]\n   562→on linking exceptions for more details.\n   563→\n   564→Finally: If you have your own fork of the OCaml distribution, it must\n   565→be distributed to your own users under the same license\n   566→(LGPL + exception), and your users will also benefit from the linking\n   567→exception.\n   568→\n   569→=== Continuous integration\n   570→\n   571→[#check-typo]\n   572→==== check-typo\n   573→\n   574→The `tools/check-typo` script enforces various typographical rules in the\n   575→OCaml compiler codebase.\n   576→\n   577→Running `./tools/check-typo` from the repository root will check all\n   578→source files. This can be fairly slow (2 minutes for example). Use\n   579→`./tools/check-typo <path>` to run it on some file or directory\n   580→(recursively) only.\n   581→\n   582→Running `./tools/check-typo-since trunk` checks all files that changed\n   583→in the commits since `trunk` -- this work with any git reference. It\n   584→runs much faster than a full `./tools/check-typo`, typically instantly.\n   585→\n   586→You can also setup a git commit-hook to automatically run `check-typo`\n   587→on the changes you commit, by copying the file\n   588→`tools/pre-commit-githook` to `.git/hooks/pre-commit`. If changes in a\n   589→commit alter the `configure` script, the hook also checks that the\n   590→committed `configure` script is up-to-date.\n   591→\n   592→Some files need special rules to opt out of `check-typo` checks; this\n   593→is specified in the `.gitattributes` file at the root of the\n   594→repository, using `typo.foo` attributes.\n   595→\n   596→===== Regenerating the `configure` script\n   597→\n   598→The `configure` script may be regenerated by running `tools/autogen`,\n   599→with GNU Autoconf installed.\n   600→\n   601→If you're using a version of GNU Autoconf that differs from our CI,\n   602→then the generated `configure` script might differ too. The latest\n   603→Ubuntu LTS packages may be used as a reasonable base to generate this\n   604→file. Using a Docker image with GNU Autoconf installed, and `git\n   605→rebase --exec`, one can properly regenerate the `configure` script for\n   606→each commit in a branch.\n   607→\n   608→[source,sh]\n   609→----\n   610→docker build -t ocaml-make-configure:latest - <<'EOF'\n   611→# syntax=docker/dockerfile:1\n   612→FROM ubuntu:latest\n   613→RUN rm -f /etc/apt/apt.conf.d/docker-clean; echo 'Binary::apt::APT::Keep-Downloaded-Packages \"true\";' > /etc/apt/apt.conf.d/keep-cache\n   614→RUN --mount=type=cache,target=/var/cache/apt,sharing=locked \\\n   615→    --mount=type=cache,target=/var/lib/apt,sharing=locked \\\n   616→    apt update && apt-get --no-install-recommends install -y autoconf make\n   617→WORKDIR /mnt\n   618→ENTRYPOINT [\"tools/autogen\"]\n   619→EOF\n   620→git rebase trunk --exec \"docker run --rm -it -v $PWD:/mnt ocaml-make-configure:latest\"\n   621→----\n   622→\n   623→==== GitHub's Continuous Integration: GitHub Actions and AppVeyor\n   624→\n   625→The scripts that are run on GitHub Actions are described in\n   626→link:.github/workflows/build.yml[].\n   627→\n   628→For example, if you want to reproduce the default build on your\n   629→machine, you can use the configuration values and run command taken from\n   630→link:tools/ci/actions/runner.sh[]:\n   631→\n   632→----\n   633→bash -ex tools/ci/actions/runner.sh configure\n   634→----\n   635→\n   636→The link:.github/workflows/hygiene.yml[] script supports other kinds of\n   637→tests which inspect the patch submitted as part of a pull request. These\n   638→tests rely on ancillary data generated by GitHub Actions which you have to\n   639→set explicitly to reproduce them locally.\n   640→\n   641→`Changes updated` checks that the link:Changes[] file has been modified\n   642→(hopefully to add a new entry). It can be disabled by including \"_(no change\n   643→entry needed)_\" in one of your commit messages -- but in general all patches\n   644→submitted should come with a Changes entry; see the guidelines in\n   645→link:CONTRIBUTING.md[].\n   646→\n   647→The Windows ports take a long time to test - INRIA's precheck service is the\n   648→best to use when all 6 Windows ports need testing for a branch, but the\n   649→AppVeyor scripts also support the other ports. The matrix is controlled by\n   650→the following environment variables, which should be set in link:appveyor.yml[]:\n   651→\n   652→- `PORT` - this must be set on each job. Either `mingw`, `msvc` or `cygwin`\n   653→  followed by `32` or `64`.\n   654→- `BOOTSTRAP_FLEXDLL` - must be set on each job. Either `true` or `false`.\n   655→  At present, must be `false` for Cygwin builds. Controls whether flexlink\n   656→  is bootstrapped as part of the test or installed from a binary archive.\n   657→- `FORCE_CYGWIN_UPGRADE`. Default: `0`. Set to `1` to force an upgrade of\n   658→  Cygwin packages as part of the build. Normally a full upgrade is only\n   659→  triggered if the packages installed require it.\n   660→- `BUILD_MODE`. Default: `world.opt`. Either `world.opt`, `steps`, or `C`.\n   661→  Controls whether the build uses the `world.opt` target or the classic\n   662→  `world`, `opt`, `opt.opt` targets. The `C` build is a fast test used to\n   663→  build just enough of the tree to cover the C sources (it's used to test\n   664→  old MSVC compilers).\n   665→- `SDK`. Defaults to Visual Studio 2022. Specifies the exact command to run\n   666→  to set-up the Microsoft build environment.\n   667→- `CYGWIN_DIST`. Default: `64`. Either `64` or `32`, selects 32-bit or 64-bit\n   668→  Cygwin as the build environment.\n   669→\n   670→==== GitHub Actions Continuous Integration (CI)\n   671→\n   672→We use GitHub Actions workflows to test the OCaml compiler and runtime\n   673→in various settings. Some tests are not run by default and are\n   674→triggered by setting a label on the pull request. See below:\n   675→\n   676→- `CI: Full matrix`: expands the test matrix considerably with additional\n   677→  configurations; useful for PRs which affect the \"systems\" side of the\n   678→  compiler;\n   679→- `CI: Skip testsuite`: turns the tesuite *off* for a PR, which can be useful\n   680→  for PRs which do not affect the compiler _at all_ or while a specific failure\n   681→  is being investigated;\n   682→- `run-crosscompiler-tests`: build various cross-compilers and test them;\n   683→- `run-thread-sanitizer`: build the distribution with the Thread\n   684→  Sanitizer (TSAN), and run the whole testsuite instrumented with TSAN;\n   685→- `run-multicoretests`: run an extensive testsuite convering multicore\n   686→  features.\n   687→\n   688→==== INRIA's Continuous Integration (CI)\n   689→\n   690→INRIA provides a Jenkins continuous integration service that OCaml\n   691→uses, see link:https://ci.inria.fr/ocaml/[]. It provides a wider\n   692→architecture support (MSVC and MinGW, a zsystems s390x machine, and\n   693→various MacOS versions) than the Travis/AppVeyor testing on github,\n   694→but only runs on commits to the trunk or release branches, not on every\n   695→PR.\n   696→\n   697→You do not need to be an INRIA employee to open an account on this\n   698→jenkins service; anyone can create an account there to access build\n   699→logs and manually restart builds. If you\n   700→would like to do this but have trouble doing it, please email\n   701→ocaml-ci-admin@inria.fr.\n   702→\n   703→To be notified by email of build failures, you can subscribe to the\n   704→ocaml-ci-notifications@inria.fr mailing list by visiting\n   705→https://sympa.inria.fr/sympa/info/ocaml-ci-notifications[its web page.]\n   706→\n   707→==== Running INRIA's CI on a publicly available git branch\n   708→\n   709→If you have suspicions that your changes may fail on exotic architectures\n   710→(they touch the build system or the backend code generator,\n   711→for example) and would like to get wider testing than github's CI\n   712→provides, it is possible to manually start INRIA's CI on arbitrary git\n   713→branches even before opening a pull request as follows:\n   714→\n   715→1. Make sure you have an account on Inria's CI as described before.\n   716→\n   717→2. Make sure you have been added to the ocaml project.\n   718→\n   719→3. Prepare a branch with the code you'd like to test, say \"mybranch\". It\n   720→is probably a good idea to make sure your branch is based on the latest\n   721→trunk.\n   722→\n   723→4. Make your branch publicly available. For instance, you can fork\n   724→OCaml's GitHub repository and then push \"mybranch\" to your fork.\n   725→\n   726→5. Visit https://ci.inria.fr/ocaml/job/precheck and log in. Click on\n   727→\"Build with parameters\".\n   728→\n   729→6. Fill in the REPO_URL and BRANCH fields as appropriate and run the build.\n   730→\n   731→7. You should receive a bunch of e-mails with the build logs for each\n   732→slave and each tested configuration (with and without flambda) attached.\n   733→\n   734→==== Changing what the CI does\n   735→\n   736→INRIA's CI \"main\" and \"precheck\" jobs run the script\n   737→tools/ci-build. In particular, when running the CI on a publicly\n   738→available branch via the \"precheck\" job as explained in the previous\n   739→section, you can edit this script to change what the CI will test.\n   740→\n   741→For instance, parallel builds are only tested for the \"trunk\"\n   742→branch. In order to use \"precheck\" to test parallel build on a custom\n   743→branch, add this at the beginning of tools/ci-build:\n   744→\n   745→----\n   746→OCAML_JOBS=10\n   747→----\n   748→\n   749→=== The `caml-commits` mailing list\n   750→\n   751→If you would like to receive email notifications of all commits made to the main\n   752→git repository, you can subscribe to the caml-commits@inria.fr mailing list by\n   753→visiting https://sympa.inria.fr/sympa/info/caml-commits[its web page.]\n   754→\n   755→[#winpthreads]\n   756→=== The `winpthreads` library for the MSVC port\n   757→\n   758→The `winpthreads` library is used to emulate `pthread` for the MSVC\n   759→port. Upstream bundles it along with all the MinGW libraries so our\n   760→`winpthreads` submodule points to `git subtree` repository rather than\n   761→upstream directly.\n   762→\n   763→To recreate the `winpthreads` repository from upstream, you can do:\n   764→\n   765→----\n   766→git clone -o upstream https://git.code.sf.net/p/mingw-w64/mingw-w64 winpthreads\n   767→cd winpthreads\n   768→git checkout upstream/master\n   769→git branch -D master\n   770→git subtree -P mingw-w64-libraries/winpthreads split -b master\n   771→----\n   772→\n   773→As subtree splitting is deterministic, repeating these operations later will\n   774→allow to update `master`, for instance by:\n   775→\n   776→----\n   777→git fetch upstream\n   778→git checkout upstream/master\n   779→git subtree -P mingw-w64-libraries/winpthreads split -b tmp\n   780→git checkout master\n   781→git merge --ff-only tmp\n   782→git branch -d tmp\n   783→----\n   784→\n   785→and then go on updating the `winpthreads` submodule in the `ocaml` repository.\n   786→\n   787→Happy Hacking!\n   788→"}]},"parent_tool_use_id":null,"session_id":"ef3f7878-6c56-44ea-b3e1-139055c7fb00","uuid":"f531ae0e-ab87-44f8-816a-119cb6c12b4c","tool_use_result":{"type":"text","file":{"filePath":"/app/ocaml/HACKING.adoc","content":"= Hacking the compiler :camel:\n\nThis document is a work-in-progress attempt to provide useful\ninformation for people willing to inspect or modify the compiler\ndistribution's codebase. Feel free to improve it by sending change\nproposals for it.\n\nIf you already have a patch that you would like to contribute to the\nofficial distribution, please see link:CONTRIBUTING.md[].\n\n=== Your first compiler modification\n\n1. Create a new git branch to store your changes.\n+\n----\ngit checkout -b my-modification\n----\nUsually, this branch wants to be based on `trunk`. If your changes must be on a\nspecific release, use its release branch (*not* the release tag) instead. For\nexample, to make a fix for 4.11.1, base your branch on *4.11* (not on *4.11.1*).\nThe `configure` step for the compiler recognises a development build from the\n`+dev` in the version number (see file `VERSION`), and release tarballs and the tagged Git commits do\nnot have this which causes some important development things to be disabled\n(ocamltest and converting C compiler warnings to errors).\n\n2. Consult link:INSTALL.adoc[] for build instructions. Here is the gist of it:\n+\n----\n./configure\nmake -j 4\n----\nIf you are on a release build and need development options, you can add\n`--enable-ocamltest` (to allow running the testsuite) and `--enable-warn-error`\n(so you don't get caught by CI later!).\n\n3. Try the newly built compiler binaries `ocamlc`, `ocamlopt` or their\n`.opt` version. To try the toplevel, use:\n+\n----\nmake runtop\n----\n\n4. Hack frenetically and keep rebuilding.\n\n5. Run the testsuite from time to time.\n+\n----\nmake tests\n----\n\n6. You did it, Well done! Consult link:CONTRIBUTING.md[] to send your contribution upstream.\n\nSee also our <<tips,development tips and tricks>>, for example on how to\n<<opam-switch,create an opam switch>> to test your modified compiler.\n\n=== What to do\n\nThere is always a lot of potential tasks, both for old and\nnewcomers. Here are various potential projects:\n\n* https://github.com/ocaml/ocaml/issues[The OCaml\n  bugtracker] contains reported bugs and feature requests. Some\n  changes that should be accessible to newcomers are marked with the\n  tag link:++https://github.com/ocaml/ocaml/issues?q=is%3Aopen+is%3Aissue+label%3Anewcomer-job++[\n  newcomer-job].\n\n* The\n  https://github.com/ocamllabs/compiler-hacking/wiki/Things-to-work-on[OCaml\n  Labs compiler-hacking wiki] contains various ideas of changes to\n  propose, some easy, some requiring a fair amount of work.\n\n* Documentation improvements are always much appreciated, either in\n  the various `.mli` files or in the official manual\n  (See link:manual/README.md[]). If you invest effort in understanding\n  a part of the codebase, submitting a pull request that adds\n  clarifying comments can be an excellent contribution to help you,\n  next time, and other code readers.\n\n* The https://github.com/ocaml/ocaml[github project] contains a lot of\n  pull requests, many of them being in dire need of a review -- we\n  have more people willing to contribute changes than to review\n  someone else's change. Picking one of them, trying to understand the\n  code (looking at the code around it) and asking questions about what\n  you don't understand or what feels odd is super-useful. It helps the\n  contribution process, and it is also an excellent way to get to know\n  various parts of the compiler from the angle of a specific aspect or\n  feature.\n+\nAgain, reviewing small or medium-sized pull requests is accessible to\nanyone with OCaml programming experience, and helps maintainers and\nother contributors. If you also submit pull requests yourself, a good\ndiscipline is to review at least as many pull requests as you submit.\n\n== Structure of the compiler\n\nThe compiler codebase can be intimidating at first sight. Here are\na few pointers to get started.\n\n=== Compilation pipeline\n\n==== The driver -- link:driver/[]\n\nThe driver contains the \"main\" function of the compilers that drive\ncompilation. It parses the command-line arguments and composes the\nrequired compiler passes by calling functions from the various parts\nof the compiler described below.\n\n==== Parsing -- link:parsing/[]\n\nParses source files and produces an Abstract Syntax Tree (AST)\n(link:parsing/parsetree.mli[] has lot of helpful comments). See\nlink:parsing/HACKING.adoc[].\n\nThe logic for Camlp4 and Ppx preprocessing is not in link:parsing/[],\nbut in link:driver/[], see link:driver/pparse.mli[] and\nlink:driver/pparse.ml[].\n\n==== Typing -- link:typing/[]\n\nType-checks the AST and produces a typed representation of the program\n(link:typing/typedtree.mli[] has some helpful comments). See\nlink:typing/HACKING.adoc[].\n\n==== The bytecode compiler -- link:bytecomp/[]\n\n==== The native compiler -- link:middle_end/[] and link:asmcomp/[]\n\n=== Runtime system\n\nThe low-level routines that OCaml programs use during their execution:\ngarbage collection, interaction with the operating system\n(IO in particular), low-level primitives to manipulate some OCaml data\nstructures, etc. Mostly implemented in C, with some rare bits of\nassembly code in architecture-specific files. The \"includes\"\ncorresponding to the `.c` files are in the link:runtime/caml[]\nsubdirectory.\n\nSome files are only used by bytecode programs, some only used by\nnative-compiled programs, but most of the runtime code is\ncommon. (See `runtime_COMMON_C_SOURCES`, `runtime_BYTECODE_ONLY_C_SOURCES`,\nand `runtime_NATIVE_ONLY_C_SOURCES` in link:Makefile[] for the list of common,\nbytecode-only, and native-only source files.)\n\nSee link:runtime/HACKING.adoc[].\n\n=== Libraries\n\nlink:stdlib/[]:: The standard library. Each file is largely\nindependent and should not need further knowledge.\n\nlink:otherlibs/[]:: External libraries such as `unix`, `threads`,\n`dynlink` and `str`.\n\nInstructions for building the full reference manual are provided in\nlink:manual/README.md[]. However, if you only modify the documentation\ncomments in `.mli` files in the compiler codebase, you can observe the\nresult by running\n\n----\nmake html_doc\n----\n\nand then opening link:./api_docgen/ocamldoc/build/html/libref/index.html[] in a web browser.\nThe documentation is located in\nlink:./api_docgen/odoc/build/html/libref/index.html[] when `--with-odoc` is\npassed to the configure script.\n\n=== Tools\n\nlink:lex/[]:: The `ocamllex` lexer generator.\n\nlink:yacc/[]:: The `ocamlyacc` parser generator. We do not recommend\nusing it for user projects in need of a parser generator. Please\nconsider using and contributing to\nlink:http://gallium.inria.fr/~fpottier/menhir/[menhir] instead, which\nhas tons of extra features, lets you write more readable grammars, and\nhas excellent documentation.\n\n=== Complete file listing\n\n  BOOTSTRAP.adoc::        instructions for bootstrapping\n  Changes::               what's new with each release\n  CONTRIBUTING.md::       how to contribute to OCaml\n  HACKING.adoc::          this file\n  INSTALL.adoc::          instructions for installation\n  LICENSE::               license and copyright notice\n  Makefile::              main Makefile\n  Makefile.common::       common Makefile definitions\n  README.adoc::           general information on the compiler distribution\n  README.win32.adoc::     general information on the Windows ports of OCaml\n  VERSION::               version string. Run `tools/autogen` after changing.\n  asmcomp/::              native-code compiler and linker\n  boot/::                 bootstrap compiler\n  build-aux/::            autotools support scripts\n  bytecomp/::             bytecode compiler and linker\n  compilerlibs/::         the OCaml compiler as a library\n  configure::             configure script\n  configure.ac::          autoconf input file\n  debugger/::             source-level replay debugger\n  driver/::               driver code for the compilers\n  flexdll/::              git submodule -- see link:README.win32.adoc[]\n  lex/::                  lexer generator\n  man/::                  man pages\n  manual/::               system to generate the manual\n  middle_end/::           the flambda optimisation phase\n  ocamldoc/::             documentation generator\n  ocamltest/::            test driver\n  otherlibs/::            several additional libraries\n  parsing/::              syntax analysis -- see link:parsing/HACKING.adoc[]\n  release-info/::         documentation and tools to prepare releases\n  runtime/::              bytecode interpreter and runtime systems\n  stdlib/::               standard library\n  testsuite/::            tests -- see link:testsuite/HACKING.adoc[]\n  tools/::                various utilities\n  toplevel/::             interactive system\n  typing/::               typechecking -- see link:typing/HACKING.adoc[]\n  utils/::                utility libraries\n  winpthreads/::          winpthreads submodule -- see <<winpthreads,further>>\n  yacc/::                 parser generator\n\n[#tips]\n== Development tips and tricks\n\n=== Keep merge commits when merging and cherry-picking Github PRs\n\nHaving the Github PR number show up in the git log is very useful for\nlater triaging. We recently disabled the \"Rebase and merge\" button,\nprecisely because it does not produce a merge commit.\n\nWhen you cherry-pick a PR in another branch, please cherry-pick this\nmerge-style commit rather than individual commits, whenever\npossible. (Picking a merge commit typically requires the `-m 1`\noption.) You should also use the `-x` option to include the hash of\nthe original commit in the commit message.\n\n----\ngit cherry-pick -x -m 1 <merge-commit-hash>\n----\n\n=== Code style\n\nKeep the style of the code you’re modifying. We don’t enforce the use of\nautomated formatters. For OCaml code,\nhttps://github.com/OCamlPro/ocp-indent[ocp-indent] has been used.\nWe use https://editorconfig.org/[EditorConfig] for simple styling. Lots of\neditors support EditorConfig\nhttps://editorconfig.org/#pre-installed[out-of-the-box], or with\nhttps://editorconfig.org/#download[plugins].\n\n[#opam-switch]\n=== Testing with `opam`\n\nIf you are working on a development version of the compiler, you can create an\nopam switch from it by running the following from the development repository:\n\n-----\nopam switch create . --empty\nopam install .\n-----\n\nIf you want to test someone else's development version from a public\ngit repository, you can build a switch directly (without cloning their\nwork locally) by pinning:\n\n----\nopam switch create my-switch-name --empty\nopam pin add ocaml-variants git+https://$REPO#branch\n----\n\n==== Incremental builds with `opam`\n\nThis section documents some tips to speed up your workflow when you need to\nalternate between testing your branch and patching the compiler.\nWe'll assume that you're currently in a clone of the compiler's source code.\n\n===== Initial setup\n\nFor the rest of the section to work, you'll need your compiler to be\nconfigured in the same way as `opam` would have configured it. The simplest\nway is to run the normal commands for the switch initialization, with the extra\n`--inplace-build` flag:\n\n-----\nopam switch create . --empty\nopam install . --inplace-build\n-----\n\nHowever, if you need specific configuration options, you can also configure it\nmanually, as long as you make sure that the configuration prefix is the one\nwhere `opam` would install the compiler.\nYou will then need to install the compiler, either from the working directory\n(that you must build yourself) or using the regular sandboxed builds.\n\n-----\n# Example with regular opam build\nopam switch create . --empty\nopam install .\n./configure --prefix=$(opam var prefix) # put extra configuration args here\n-----\n\n-----\n# Example with installation from the current directory\nopam switch create . --empty\n./configure --prefix=$(opam var prefix) # put extra configuration args here\nmake -j\nopam install . --assume-built\n-----\n\n===== Basic workflow\n\nWe will assume that the workflow alternates between work on the compiler and\nexternal (`opam`-related) commands.\nAs an example, debugging an issue in the compiler can be done by a first step\nthat triggers the issue (by installing a given `opam` package), then adding\nsome logging to the compiler, re-trigger the issue, and based on the logs either\nadd more logging, or try a patch, and so on.\n\nThe part of this workflow that we're going to optimize is when we switch from\nworking on the compiler to using the compiler. The basic way to do this is to\nrun `opam install .` again, but this will recompile the compiler from scratch\nand also trigger a recompilation of all the packages in the switch.\n\n===== Using `opam-custom-install`\n\nThe `opam-custom-install` plugin allows you to install a package using a custom\ncommand instead of the package-supplied one. It can be installed following\ninstructions https://gitlab.ocamlpro.com/louis/opam-custom-install[here].\n\nIn our case, we need to build the compiler, and when we've built everything\nthat we need then we run `opam custom-install ocaml-variants -- make install`.\nThis will make `opam` remove the previously installed version of the compiler\n(if any), then install the new one in its stead.\n\n-----\n# reinstall the compiler, and rebuild all opam packages\nopam custom-install ocaml-variants -- make install\n-----\n\nSince most `opam` packages depend on the compiler, this will trigger a\nreinstallation of all the packages in the switch.\nIf you want to avoid that (for instance, your patch only adds some logging\nso you expect the core libraries and all the already compiled packages to be\nidentical), you can use the additional `--no-recompilations` flag.\nThere are no checks that it's safe to do so, so if your patch ends up\nchanging even slightly one of the core libraries' files, you will likely\nget inconsistent assumptions errors later.\n\n-----\n# reinstall the compiler, leaving the opam packages untouched -- unsafe!\nopam custom-install --no-recompilations ocaml-variants -- make install\n-----\n\nNote about the first installation:\nWhen you start from an empty switch, and install a compiler (in our case,\nthe `ocaml-variants` package provided by the compiler's `opam` file), then\na number of additional packages are installed to ensure that the switch\nwill work correctly. Mainly, the `ocaml` package needs to be installed,\nand while it's done automatically when using regular `opam` commands, the\n`custom-install` plugin will not force installation of dependencies.\nMoreover, if you try to fix the problem by manually installing the `ocaml`\npackage, `opam` will try to recompile `ocaml-variants`, using the default\ninstructions. You can get around this by running\n`opam reinstall --forget-pending` just after the `opam custom-install` command\nand just before the `opam install ocaml command`.\nFull example:\n\n-----\nopam switch create . --empty\n./configure --prefix=$(opam var prefix) --disable-ocamldoc --disable-ocamltest\nmake world && make opt\nopam custom-install ocaml-variants -- make install\nopam reinstall --forget-pending --yes\nopam install ocaml\n# You now have a working switch, in which you can start installing packages\n-----\n\nOne advantage of this plugin over a plain `make install` is that it\ncorrectly tracks the files associated with the compiler, so if your\n`make install` command only installs the bytecode versions of the tools,\nthen with `opam-custom-install` you will end up in a state where only the\nbytecode tools are installed, whereas with a raw `make install` you will have\nstale native binaries remaining in your switch.\nSince it's significantly faster to build the bytecode version of the tools,\nand many `opam` packages will pick the native version of the compilers if\npresent and the bytecode version otherwise, you can build your initial switch\nwith the native versions (to get quickly to a state where a bug appears),\nthen clean your working directory and start building bytecode tools only\nfor the actual debugging phase.\n\n===== Without `opam-custom-install`\n\nYou can achieve some improvements using built-in `opam` commands.\n\nUsing `opam install . --assume-built` will simply remove the\npackage for the compiler, then run the installation instructions\n(`make install`) in the working directory, tracking the installed files\ncorrectly. The main difference with the `opam-custom-install` version\nis that there's no way to prevent this command from triggering a full\nrecompilation of your switch.\n\nYou can also run `make install` manually, which will not trigger a\nrecompilation, but will not remove the previous version either and can\nmess with `opam`'s tracking of installed files.\n\n=== Useful Makefile targets and options\n\nBesides the targets listed in link:INSTALL.adoc[] for build and\ninstallation, the following targets may be of use:\n\n`make runtop` :: builds and runs the ocaml toplevel of the distribution\n                          (optionally uses `rlwrap` for readline+history support)\n                          (use `make runtop-with-otherlibs` if you need `Unix` or other\n                           `otherlibs/` libraries)\n`make natruntop`:: builds and runs the native ocaml toplevel (experimental)\n\n`make partialclean`:: Clean the OCaml files but keep the compiled C files.\n\n`make depend`:: Regenerate the `.depend` file. Should be used each time new dependencies are added between files.\n\n`make -C testsuite parallel`:: see link:testsuite/HACKING.adoc[]\n\nYou can use `make foo V=1` to build the target foo and show full\ncommands instead of abbreviated names like OCAMLC, etc. This can be\nuseful to know the flags to use to manually rebuild a file.\n\nAdditionally, there are some developer specific targets in link:Makefile.dev[].\nThese targets are automatically available when working in a Git clone of the\nrepository, but are not available from a tarball.\n\n=== Automatic configure options\n\nIf you have options to `configure` which you always (or at least frequently)\nuse, it's possible to store them in Git, and `configure` will automatically add\nthem. For example, you may wish to avoid building the debug runtime by default\nwhile developing, in which case you can issue\n`git config --global ocaml.configure '--disable-debug-runtime'`. The `configure`\nscript will alert you that it has picked up this option and added it _before_\nany options you specified for `configure`.\n\nOptions are added before those passed on the command line, so it's possible to\noverride them, for example `./configure --enable-debug-runtime` will build the\ndebug runtime, since the enable flag appears after the disable flag. You can\nalso use the full power of Git's `config` command and have options specific to\nparticular clone or worktree.\n\n=== Speeding up configure\n\n`configure` includes the standard `-C` option which caches various test results\nin the file `config.cache` and can use those results to avoid running tests in\nsubsequent invocations. This mechanism works fine, except that it is easy to\nclean the cache by mistake (e.g. with `git clean -dfX`). The cache is also\nhost-specific which means the file has to be deleted if you run `configure` with\na new `--host` value (this is quite common on Windows, where `configure` is\nalso quite slow to run).\n\nYou can elect to have host-specific cache files by issuing\n`git config --global ocaml.configure-cache .`. The `configure` script will now\nautomatically create `ocaml-host.cache` (e.g. `ocaml-x86_64-pc-windows.cache`,\nor `ocaml-default.cache`). If you work with multiple worktrees, you can share\nthese cache files by issuing `git config --global ocaml.configure-cache ..`. The\ndirectory is interpreted _relative_ to the `configure` script.\n\n=== Bootstrapping\n\nThe OCaml compiler is bootstrapped. This means that\npreviously-compiled bytecode versions of the compiler and lexer are\nincluded in the repository under the\nlink:boot/[] directory. These bytecode images are used once the\nbytecode runtime (which is written in C) has been built to compile the\nstandard library and then to build a fresh compiler. Details can be\nfound in link:BOOTSTRAP.adoc[].\n\n=== Speeding up builds\n\nOnce you've built a natively-compiled `ocamlc.opt`, you can use it to\nspeed up future builds by copying it to `boot`:\n\n----\ncp ocamlc.opt boot/\n----\n\nIf `boot/ocamlc` changes (e.g. because you ran `make bootstrap`), then\nthe build will revert to the slower bytecode-compiled `ocamlc` until\nyou do the above step again.\n\n=== Using merlin\n\nDuring the development of the compiler, the internal format of compiled object\nfiles evolves, and quickly becomes incompatible with the format of the last\nOCaml release. In particular, even an up-to-date merlin will be unable to use\nthem during most of the development cycle: opening a compiler source file with\nmerlin gives a frustrating error message.\n\nTo use merlin on the compiler, you want to build the compiler with an older\nversion of itself. One easy way to do this is to use the experimental build\nrules for Dune, which are distributed with the compiler (with no guarantees that\nthe build will work all the time). Assuming you already have a recent OCaml\nversion installed with merlin and dune, you can just run the following from the\ncompiler sources:\n\n----\n./configure # if not already done\nmake clean && dune build @libs\n----\n\nwhich will do a bytecode build of all the distribution (without linking\nthe executables), using your OCaml compiler.\n\nMerlin will be looking at the artefacts generated by dune (in `_build`), rather\nthan trying to open the incompatible artefacts produced by a Makefile build. In\nparticular, you need to repeat the dune build every time you change the interface\nof some compilation unit, so that merlin is aware of the new interface.\n\nYou only need to run `configure` once, but you will need to run `make clean`\nevery time you want to run `dune` after you built something with `make`;\notherwise dune will complain that build artefacts are present among the sources.\n\nFinally, there will be times where the compiler simply cannot be built with an\nolder version of itself. One example of this is when a new primitive is added to\nthe runtime, and then used in the standard library straight away, since the rest\nof the compiler requires the `stdlib` library to build, nothing can be build. In\nsuch situations, you will have to either live without merlin, or develop on an\nolder branch of the compiler, for example the maintenance branch of the last\nreleased version. Developing a patch from a release branch can later introduce a\nsubstantial amount of extra work, when you rebase to the current development\nversion. But it also makes it a lot easier to test the impact of your work on\nthird-party code, by installing a local <<opam-switch,opam switch>>: opam\npackages tend to be compatible with released versions of the compiler, whereas\nmost packages are incompatible with the in-progress development version.\n\n\n=== License\n\nThe OCaml distribution in this repository, which includes the OCaml\ncompiler and runtime, the OCaml standard library, and some extra\ntools, is licensed under the LGPL version 2.1, with a linking\nexception. See link:LICENSE[] for the full license text. Using the\nhttps://spdx.org/licenses/[SPDX] standard for license identifiers,\nthis is the license \"LGPL-2.1 WITH\nhttps://spdx.org/licenses/OCaml-LGPL-linking-exception.html[OCaml-LGPL-linking-exception]\".\nLet us include here a few explanations about the intent of this\nlicensing choice -- note that only the license text has legal value.\n\nThe broad intent of the LGPL is that if you modify the OCaml\ndistribution, and distribute your modified version to your users, then\nyou have to provide the source code of your version -- the LGPL, like\nthe GPL and unlike the BSD and MIT licenses, is \"hereditary\"; modified\nversions of LGPL programs must remain free software under the same\nlicense. (For more general discussion of the LGPL license, see the\nhttps://www.gnu.org/licenses/gpl-faq.en.html[FAQ] maintained by the\nGNU project.)\n\nOn the other hand, if your code is not derived from the OCaml\ndistribution, it only uses it, then you can use the licensing terms of\nyour choice. We use an explicit \"linking exception\" precisely to make\nit clear that it is fine to link statically or dynamically with parts\nof the OCaml distribution, typically the OCaml runtime and the\nstandard library. Linking exceptions are a common practice for\nlanguage compilers and runtime libraries, for example the GCC project\nuses such an exception. See the\nhttps://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/GPL_linking_exception[Wikipedia article]\non linking exceptions for more details.\n\nFinally: If you have your own fork of the OCaml distribution, it must\nbe distributed to your own users under the same license\n(LGPL + exception), and your users will also benefit from the linking\nexception.\n\n=== Continuous integration\n\n[#check-typo]\n==== check-typo\n\nThe `tools/check-typo` script enforces various typographical rules in the\nOCaml compiler codebase.\n\nRunning `./tools/check-typo` from the repository root will check all\nsource files. This can be fairly slow (2 minutes for example). Use\n`./tools/check-typo <path>` to run it on some file or directory\n(recursively) only.\n\nRunning `./tools/check-typo-since trunk` checks all files that changed\nin the commits since `trunk` -- this work with any git reference. It\nruns much faster than a full `./tools/check-typo`, typically instantly.\n\nYou can also setup a git commit-hook to automatically run `check-typo`\non the changes you commit, by copying the file\n`tools/pre-commit-githook` to `.git/hooks/pre-commit`. If changes in a\ncommit alter the `configure` script, the hook also checks that the\ncommitted `configure` script is up-to-date.\n\nSome files need special rules to opt out of `check-typo` checks; this\nis specified in the `.gitattributes` file at the root of the\nrepository, using `typo.foo` attributes.\n\n===== Regenerating the `configure` script\n\nThe `configure` script may be regenerated by running `tools/autogen`,\nwith GNU Autoconf installed.\n\nIf you're using a version of GNU Autoconf that differs from our CI,\nthen the generated `configure` script might differ too. The latest\nUbuntu LTS packages may be used as a reasonable base to generate this\nfile. Using a Docker image with GNU Autoconf installed, and `git\nrebase --exec`, one can properly regenerate the `configure` script for\neach commit in a branch.\n\n[source,sh]\n----\ndocker build -t ocaml-make-configure:latest - <<'EOF'\n# syntax=docker/dockerfile:1\nFROM ubuntu:latest\nRUN rm -f /etc/apt/apt.conf.d/docker-clean; echo 'Binary::apt::APT::Keep-Downloaded-Packages \"true\";' > /etc/apt/apt.conf.d/keep-cache\nRUN --mount=type=cache,target=/var/cache/apt,sharing=locked \\\n    --mount=type=cache,target=/var/lib/apt,sharing=locked \\\n    apt update && apt-get --no-install-recommends install -y autoconf make\nWORKDIR /mnt\nENTRYPOINT [\"tools/autogen\"]\nEOF\ngit rebase trunk --exec \"docker run --rm -it -v $PWD:/mnt ocaml-make-configure:latest\"\n----\n\n==== GitHub's Continuous Integration: GitHub Actions and AppVeyor\n\nThe scripts that are run on GitHub Actions are described in\nlink:.github/workflows/build.yml[].\n\nFor example, if you want to reproduce the default build on your\nmachine, you can use the configuration values and run command taken from\nlink:tools/ci/actions/runner.sh[]:\n\n----\nbash -ex tools/ci/actions/runner.sh configure\n----\n\nThe link:.github/workflows/hygiene.yml[] script supports other kinds of\ntests which inspect the patch submitted as part of a pull request. These\ntests rely on ancillary data generated by GitHub Actions which you have to\nset explicitly to reproduce them locally.\n\n`Changes updated` checks that the link:Changes[] file has been modified\n(hopefully to add a new entry). It can be disabled by including \"_(no change\nentry needed)_\" in one of your commit messages -- but in general all patches\nsubmitted should come with a Changes entry; see the guidelines in\nlink:CONTRIBUTING.md[].\n\nThe Windows ports take a long time to test - INRIA's precheck service is the\nbest to use when all 6 Windows ports need testing for a branch, but the\nAppVeyor scripts also support the other ports. The matrix is controlled by\nthe following environment variables, which should be set in link:appveyor.yml[]:\n\n- `PORT` - this must be set on each job. Either `mingw`, `msvc` or `cygwin`\n  followed by `32` or `64`.\n- `BOOTSTRAP_FLEXDLL` - must be set on each job. Either `true` or `false`.\n  At present, must be `false` for Cygwin builds. Controls whether flexlink\n  is bootstrapped as part of the test or installed from a binary archive.\n- `FORCE_CYGWIN_UPGRADE`. Default: `0`. Set to `1` to force an upgrade of\n  Cygwin packages as part of the build. Normally a full upgrade is only\n  triggered if the packages installed require it.\n- `BUILD_MODE`. Default: `world.opt`. Either `world.opt`, `steps`, or `C`.\n  Controls whether the build uses the `world.opt` target or the classic\n  `world`, `opt`, `opt.opt` targets. The `C` build is a fast test used to\n  build just enough of the tree to cover the C sources (it's used to test\n  old MSVC compilers).\n- `SDK`. Defaults to Visual Studio 2022. Specifies the exact command to run\n  to set-up the Microsoft build environment.\n- `CYGWIN_DIST`. Default: `64`. Either `64` or `32`, selects 32-bit or 64-bit\n  Cygwin as the build environment.\n\n==== GitHub Actions Continuous Integration (CI)\n\nWe use GitHub Actions workflows to test the OCaml compiler and runtime\nin various settings. Some tests are not run by default and are\ntriggered by setting a label on the pull request. See below:\n\n- `CI: Full matrix`: expands the test matrix considerably with additional\n  configurations; useful for PRs which affect the \"systems\" side of the\n  compiler;\n- `CI: Skip testsuite`: turns the tesuite *off* for a PR, which can be useful\n  for PRs which do not affect the compiler _at all_ or while a specific failure\n  is being investigated;\n- `run-crosscompiler-tests`: build various cross-compilers and test them;\n- `run-thread-sanitizer`: build the distribution with the Thread\n  Sanitizer (TSAN), and run the whole testsuite instrumented with TSAN;\n- `run-multicoretests`: run an extensive testsuite convering multicore\n  features.\n\n==== INRIA's Continuous Integration (CI)\n\nINRIA provides a Jenkins continuous integration service that OCaml\nuses, see link:https://ci.inria.fr/ocaml/[]. It provides a wider\narchitecture support (MSVC and MinGW, a zsystems s390x machine, and\nvarious MacOS versions) than the Travis/AppVeyor testing on github,\nbut only runs on commits to the trunk or release branches, not on every\nPR.\n\nYou do not need to be an INRIA employee to open an account on this\njenkins service; anyone can create an account there to access build\nlogs and manually restart builds. If you\nwould like to do this but have trouble doing it, please email\nocaml-ci-admin@inria.fr.\n\nTo be notified by email of build failures, you can subscribe to the\nocaml-ci-notifications@inria.fr mailing list by visiting\nhttps://sympa.inria.fr/sympa/info/ocaml-ci-notifications[its web page.]\n\n==== Running INRIA's CI on a publicly available git branch\n\nIf you have suspicions that your changes may fail on exotic architectures\n(they touch the build system or the backend code generator,\nfor example) and would like to get wider testing than github's CI\nprovides, it is possible to manually start INRIA's CI on arbitrary git\nbranches even before opening a pull request as follows:\n\n1. Make sure you have an account on Inria's CI as described before.\n\n2. Make sure you have been added to the ocaml project.\n\n3. Prepare a branch with the code you'd like to test, say \"mybranch\". It\nis probably a good idea to make sure your branch is based on the latest\ntrunk.\n\n4. Make your branch publicly available. For instance, you can fork\nOCaml's GitHub repository and then push \"mybranch\" to your fork.\n\n5. Visit https://ci.inria.fr/ocaml/job/precheck and log in. Click on\n\"Build with parameters\".\n\n6. Fill in the REPO_URL and BRANCH fields as appropriate and run the build.\n\n7. You should receive a bunch of e-mails with the build logs for each\nslave and each tested configuration (with and without flambda) attached.\n\n==== Changing what the CI does\n\nINRIA's CI \"main\" and \"precheck\" jobs run the script\ntools/ci-build. In particular, when running the CI on a publicly\navailable branch via the \"precheck\" job as explained in the previous\nsection, you can edit this script to change what the CI will test.\n\nFor instance, parallel builds are only tested for the \"trunk\"\nbranch. In order to use \"precheck\" to test parallel build on a custom\nbranch, add this at the beginning of tools/ci-build:\n\n----\nOCAML_JOBS=10\n----\n\n=== The `caml-commits` mailing list\n\nIf you would like to receive email notifications of all commits made to the main\ngit repository, you can subscribe to the caml-commits@inria.fr mailing list by\nvisiting https://sympa.inria.fr/sympa/info/caml-commits[its web page.]\n\n[#winpthreads]\n=== The `winpthreads` library for the MSVC port\n\nThe `winpthreads` library is used to emulate `pthread` for the MSVC\nport. Upstream bundles it along with all the MinGW libraries so our\n`winpthreads` submodule points to `git subtree` repository rather than\nupstream directly.\n\nTo recreate the `winpthreads` repository from upstream, you can do:\n\n----\ngit clone -o upstream https://git.code.sf.net/p/mingw-w64/mingw-w64 winpthreads\ncd winpthreads\ngit checkout upstream/master\ngit branch -D master\ngit subtree -P mingw-w64-libraries/winpthreads split -b master\n----\n\nAs subtree splitting is deterministic, repeating these operations later will\nallow to update `master`, for instance by:\n\n----\ngit fetch upstream\ngit checkout upstream/master\ngit subtree -P mingw-w64-libraries/winpthreads split -b tmp\ngit checkout master\ngit merge --ff-only tmp\ngit branch -d tmp\n----\n\nand then go on updating the `winpthreads` submodule in the `ocaml` repository.\n\nHappy Hacking!\n","numLines":788,"startLine":1,"totalLines":788}}}
